2:31:1831:0:14:72:478:700:12:Times:12:Times:C:Income and Wealth Distribution in Macroeconomics: A Continuous-Time Approach
2:32:1907:14:14:72:489:686:12:Times:12:Times:C:Yves Achdou, Jiequn Han, Jean-Michel Lasry, Pierre-Louis Lions, and Benjamin Moll
2:33:1988:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:C:NBER Working Paper No. 23732
2:34:2016:14:14:72:134:658:12:Times:12:Times:C:August 2017
2:35:2027:14:28:72:180:644:12:Times:12:Times:C:JEL No. D31,E00,E21
2:36:2046:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:C:ABSTRACT
2:37:2054:28:14:72:546:588:12:Times:12:Times:C:We  recast  the  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett  model  of  income  and  wealth  distribution  in  continuous 
2:38:2157:14:14:72:545:574:12:Times:12:Times:C:time. This workhorse model – as well as heterogeneous agent models more generally – then boils 
2:39:2251:14:14:72:546:560:12:Times:12:Times:C:down to a system of partial differential equations, a fact we take advantage of to make two types 
2:40:2348:14:14:72:545:546:12:Times:12:Times:C:of contributions. First, a number of new theoretical results: (i) an analytic characterization of the 
2:41:2449:14:14:72:545:532:12:Times:12:Times:C:consumption  and  saving behavior  of  the  poor,  particularly  their  marginal  propensities  to 
2:42:2547:14:14:72:545:518:12:Times:12:Times:C:consume; (ii) a closed-form solution for the wealth distribution in a special case with two income 
2:43:2645:14:14:72:546:504:12:Times:12:Times:C:types;  (iii)  a  proof  that  there  is  a  unique stationary  equilibrium  if  the  intertemporal  elasticity  of 
2:44:2760:14:14:72:545:490:12:Times:12:Times:C:substitution  is  weakly  greater  than  one;  (iv) characterization  of  “soft”  borrowing  constraints. 
2:45:2865:14:14:72:546:476:12:Times:12:Times:C:Second,  we  develop  a  simple,  efficient  and  portable algorithm  for  numerically  solving  for 
2:46:2965:14:14:72:545:462:12:Times:12:Times:C:equilibria  in  a  wide  class  of  heterogeneous  agent  models,  including – but  not  limited  to  –  the 
2:47:3072:14:28:72:233:448:12:Times:12:Times:C:Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model.
2:48:3102:28:14:72:138:420:12:Times:12:Times:C:Yves Achdou

2:78:3729:14:14:351:403:266:12:Times:12:Times:A:and NBER
2:79:3737:14:0:351:450:252:12:Times:12:Times:A:moll@princeton.edu
3:80:3755:0:31:72:179:720:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:C:Introduction
3:81:3767:31:17:72:540:689:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:One of the key developments in macroeconomics research over the last three decades has been
3:82:3858:17:17:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the incorporation of explicit heterogeneity into models of the macroeconomy.  Fueled by the
3:83:3949:17:17:72:540:655:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:increasing availability of high-quality micro data,  the advent of more powerful computing
3:84:4039:17:16:72:540:638:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:methods as well as rising inequality in many advanced economies, such heterogeneous agent
3:85:4128:16:17:72:540:622:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:models  have  proliferated  and  are  now  ubiquitous.   This  is  a  welcome  development  for  a
3:86:4226:17:17:72:540:605:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:number of reasons.  First, it opens up the door to bringing micro data to the table in order
3:87:4318:17:17:72:540:588:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to empirically discipline macro theories.  Second, macroeconomists often want to analyze the
3:88:4410:17:16:72:540:571:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:welfare implications of particular shocks or policies.  This is impossible without asking “who
3:89:4504:16:17:72:540:555:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:gains and who loses?”, that is, distributional considerations often cannot be ignored.  Third,
3:90:4598:17:17:72:540:538:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:models with heterogeneity often deliver strikingly different aggregate implications than do
3:91:4689:17:20:72:515:521:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:representative agent models, for example with respect to monetary and fiscal policies.1
3:92:4776:20:17:89:539:501:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Despite the continuously increasing popularity of macroeconomic models with rich het-
3:93:4861:17:16:71:539:484:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:erogeneity,  the  literature  has  suffered  from  a  dearth  of  theoretical  and  analytical  results.
3:94:4965:16:17:71:539:468:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Little is known about the properties of consumption and saving behavior in the presence of
3:95:5055:17:17:71:539:451:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:borrowing constraints, those of the resulting wealth distribution, and equilibrium uniqueness
3:96:5148:17:17:71:539:434:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(or lack thereof).  Instead, most studies rely on purely numerical analyses to characterize the
3:97:5243:17:16:71:539:417:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:implications of such theories.  But even such computational approaches are often difficult
3:98:5333:16:17:71:539:401:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and costly, particularly if the question at hand requires solving for the economy’s transition
3:99:5427:17:20:71:454:384:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:dynamics or if the model features non-differentiabilities or non-convexities.
3:100:5504:20:17:89:539:364:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:In this paper we make some progress on these issues by recasting the standard incomplete
3:101:5592:17:16:71:539:347:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:market model ofAiyagari(1994),Bewley(1986) andHuggett(1993) in continuous time. Our
3:102:5675:16:17:72:540:331:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:main contributions are twofold.  First, we prove a number of new theoretical results about
3:103:5765:17:17:72:539:314:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:this workhorse model.2Second,  we develop a simple,  efficient and portable algorithm for
3:104:5854:17:17:72:540:297:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:numerically  solving  both  stationary  equilibria  and  transition  dynamics  of  a  wide  class  of
3:105:5955:17:17:72:540:280:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:heterogeneous agent models, including – but not limited to – the Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett
3:106:6043:17:19:72:106:263:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:model.
3:107:6049:19:17:89:539:244:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Both types of contributions make use of an important property: when recast in continuous
3:108:6137:17:17:72:540:227:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time, heterogeneous agent models boil down to systems of two coupled partial differential
3:109:6226:17:17:72:540:210:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equations.  The first of these is a Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman (HJB) equation for the optimal
3:110:6316:17:16:72:540:193:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:choices of a single atomistic individual who takes the evolution of the distribution and hence
3:111:6410:16:19:72:540:177:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:prices  as  given.   And  the  second  is  a  Kolmogorov  Forward  (KF)  equation  characterizing
3:112:6507:19:15:85:539:158:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:1Deaton  (2016)  succinctly  summarizes  the  second  and  third  reasons:  “While  we  often  must  focus  on
3:113:6617:15:12:72:540:143:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:aggregates for macroeconomic policy, it is impossible to think coherently about national well-being while ig-
3:114:6726:12:12:72:540:131:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:noring inequality and poverty, neither of which is visible in aggregate data.  Indeed, and except in exceptional
3:115:6838:12:9:72:376:119:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:cases, macroeconomic aggregates themselves depend on distribution.”
3:116:6905:9:15:85:539:110:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:2Of course and as is well-known, the unadorned Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model is not sufficiently rich to
3:117:7009:15:12:72:540:95:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:be an empirically realistic theory of income and wealth distribution.  Understanding its theoretical properties
3:118:7120:12:0:72:514:83:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:is nevertheless important, simply because it forms the backbone of much of modern macroeconomics.
4:119:7218:0:16:72:539:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the  evolution  of  the  distribution,  given  optimal  choices  of  individuals.3In  the  context  of
4:120:7320:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett  model,  the  HJB  equation  characterizes  individuals’  optimal
4:121:7414:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:consumption and saving behavior given a stochastic process for income; and the KF equation
4:122:7504:17:17:72:540:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:characterizes the evolution of the joint distribution of income and wealth.  The two equations
4:123:7598:17:17:72:540:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:are coupled because optimal consumption and saving depend on the interest rate which is
4:124:7685:17:16:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:determined in equilibrium and hence depends on the wealth distribution.  We start with a
4:125:7773:16:17:72:539:620:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:particularly  parsimonious  case:  aHuggett(1993)  economy  in  which  idiosyncratic  income
4:126:7865:17:17:72:540:603:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:risk takes the form of exogenous endowment shocks that follow a two-state Poisson process
4:127:7954:17:17:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and in which individuals save in unproductive bonds that are in fixed supply.  Later in the
4:128:8045:17:16:72:539:569:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:paper, we extend many of our results to more general stochastic processes and to anAiyagari
4:129:8136:16:17:72:540:553:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(1994) economy in which individuals save in productive capital and income takes the form
4:130:8224:17:20:72:155:536:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of labor income.
4:131:8240:20:17:89:539:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:We prove four new theoretical results about the Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model.First,
4:132:8324:17:17:72:540:499:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we provide an analytic characterization of the consumption and saving behavior of the poor.
4:133:8415:17:16:72:540:482:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:We show that, under natural assumptions,4an individual’s saving policy function behaves
4:134:8502:16:0:72:102:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f11:B:like−
4:135:8507:0:10:103:113:476:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:A:√
4:136:8508:10:17:113:539:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:2νain the vicinity of the borrowing constraint whereais her wealth in deviations
4:137:8588:17:17:72:539:449:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:from this constraint andνis a constant that depends on parameters.  Equivalently, her con-
4:138:8678:17:0:72:347:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sumption function behaves like her total income plus
4:139:8730:0:10:351:361:442:12:g_d0_f11:12:g_d0_f11:A:√
4:140:8731:10:17:361:540:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:2νa.  This characterization implies
4:141:8766:17:16:72:540:415:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:that: (i) individuals necessarily hit the borrowing constraint in finite time after a long enough
4:142:8863:16:17:72:539:399:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sequence of low income shocks, (ii) we have an intuitive characterization of the speedνat
4:143:8952:17:17:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:which an individual does so as well as hermarginal propensity to consume(MPC) out of a
4:144:9038:17:17:72:540:365:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:windfall income gain.  This MPC is higher the lower is the interest rate relative to the rate of
4:145:9134:17:16:72:540:348:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time preference, the more willing to intertemporally substitute individuals are, or the higher
4:146:9228:16:17:72:540:332:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is the likelihood of getting a high income draw; it is non-monotone in the income received
4:147:9318:17:17:72:540:315:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in low-income states (e.g.  unemployment benefits).  Understanding the theoretical determi-
4:148:9409:17:17:72:540:298:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:nants of MPCs is, of course, important for a large body of applied work.5Second, we derive
4:149:9499:17:17:72:540:281:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:an analytic solution for the wealth distribution for a special case with two income types. This
4:150:9594:17:16:72:540:264:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:analytic solution provides a clean characterization of various properties of the wealth distri-
4:151:9689:16:18:72:540:248:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:bution, particularly the behavior of its left and right tails.  For example, a direct corollary
4:152:9784:18:16:85:539:230:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:3Lasry and Lions(2007) have termed such systems “Mean Field Games” in analogy to the continuum
4:153:9878:16:12:72:540:214:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:limit often taken in statistical mechanics and physics, e.g.  when solving the Ising model.  We build on their
4:154:9988:12:12:72:539:202:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:earlier work.  Also seeGu ́eant,  Lasry,  and Lions(2011) andCardialaguet(2013) and the references cited
4:155:10092:12:12:72:540:190:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:therein.  The “Kolmogorov Forward equation” is also often called “Fokker-Planck equation.” Because the
4:156:10194:12:12:72:540:178:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:term “Kolmogorov Forward equation” seems to be somewhat more widely used in economics, we will use
4:157:10292:12:8:72:518:166:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:this convention throughout the paper.  But these are really two different names for the same equation.
4:158:10394:8:15:85:539:158:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:4Namely  that  either  the  borrowing  constraint  is  tighter  than  the  natural  borrowing  constraint  or  the
4:159:10508:15:9:72:464:143:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:coefficient of absolute risk aversion is bounded as consumption approaches zero (or both).
4:160:10598:9:15:85:539:134:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:5The  distribution  of  MPCs  determines,  for  example,  the  efficacy  of  fiscal  stimulus  (e.g.Kaplan  and
4:161:10709:15:12:72:539:119:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:Violante,2014;Hagedorn, Manovski, and Mitman,2017), the transition mechanism of monetary policy (e.g.
4:162:10810:12:12:72:540:107:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:Auclert,2017;Kaplan, Moll, and Violante,2016), the effect of a credit crunch or house price movements on
4:163:10914:12:12:72:539:95:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:consumer spending (e.g.Guerrieri and Lorenzoni,2017;Berger, Guerrieri, Lorenzoni, and Vavra,2015) and
4:164:11015:12:0:72:488:83:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:the extent to which inequality affects aggregate demand (e.g.Auclert and Rognlie,2016,2017).
5:165:11108:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of individuals hitting the borrowing constraint in finite time is that the wealth distribution
5:166:11202:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:features a Dirac point mass at this constraint.Third, we prove existence and uniqueness of a
5:167:11294:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stationary equilibrium for general utility functions and income processes under the intuitive
5:168:11387:17:17:72:540:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:condition that the intertemporal elasticity of substitution (IES)−u′(c)/(u′′(c)c) is weakly
5:169:11478:17:17:72:539:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:greater than one for all consumption levelsc.6Without a uniqueness result the economy
5:170:11563:17:16:72:539:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:could, in principle, be subject to poverty traps and history dependence.7Fourth, we con-
5:171:11651:16:17:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sider “soft borrowing constraints”,  i.e.  a wedge between borrowing and saving rates,  and
5:172:11742:17:17:72:540:603:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:characterize their implication for saving behavior and the wealth distribution.  This form of
5:173:11835:17:17:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:constraint can explain the empirical observation that wealth distributions typically have a
5:174:11926:17:19:72:437:569:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:spike at zero net worth and mass both to the left and the right of zero.
5:175:11998:19:17:89:539:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:In  addition  to  these  results,  which  are  new  also  relative  to  the  existing  discrete-time
5:176:12098:17:17:72:540:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:literature, we extend some useful existing discrete-time results and concepts to continuous
5:177:12189:17:17:72:539:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time.   First,  we  adapt  a  number  of  results  fromAiyagari(1994),  e.g.   that  the  wealth
5:178:12285:17:17:72:540:499:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:distribution has a finite upper bound and that a stationary equilibrium exists.  Second, we
5:179:12376:17:16:72:540:482:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:characterize the saving behavior of the wealthy and show that, with constant relative risk
5:180:12466:16:17:72:540:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:aversion (CRRA) utility,  consumption and saving policy functions become linear for high
5:181:12554:17:17:72:539:449:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wealth (Benhabib, Bisin, and Zhu,2015;Benhabib and Bisin,2016).  Third, we show how
5:182:12637:17:17:72:540:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to define in continuous time marginal propensities to consume and save over discrete time
5:183:12726:17:16:72:540:415:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:intervals.  This is not obvious and, at the same time, important for bringing the model to the
5:184:12820:16:17:72:540:399:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:data. Finally, a methodological contribution of our paper is to show how to handle borrowing
5:185:12912:17:17:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:constraints  in  continuous  time:  conveniently,  the  borrowing  constraint  never  binds  in  the
5:186:13012:17:17:72:540:365:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:interior of the state space and only shows up in a boundary condition.8The consumption
5:187:13098:17:16:72:540:348:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:first-order condition always holds with equality, thereby sidestepping any complications due
5:188:13190:16:20:72:454:332:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to “occasionally binding constraints.” Many of our proofs exploit this fact.
5:189:13266:20:17:89:539:312:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:As  already  mentioned,  our  second  main  contribution  is  the  development  of  a  simple,
5:190:13360:17:17:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:efficient and portable numerical algorithm for computing a wide class of heterogeneous agent
5:191:13452:17:17:72:540:278:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:models.  The algorithm is based on a finite difference method and applies to the computation
5:192:13544:17:16:72:539:261:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of both stationary and time-varying equilibria.9We explain this algorithm in the context
5:193:13632:16:18:72:540:245:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of  the  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett  model.   But  the  algorithm  is,  in  fact,  considerably  more
5:194:13730:18:15:85:539:227:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:6In addition we assume for the uniqueness result that individuals cannot borrow.  A key step in our proof
5:195:13835:15:12:72:540:212:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:is an important result by Olivi (2017).  Contemporaneous work by Light (2017) derives a uniqueness result
5:196:13940:12:12:72:540:200:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:under more restrictive assumptions in a discrete-time setting:  he proves uniqueness for an Aiyagari economy
5:197:14048:12:12:72:540:188:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:with CRRA utility and Cobb-Douglas production (as well as no borrowing) under the assumption that the
5:198:14149:12:8:72:216:176:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:constant IES is greater than one.
5:199:14182:8:16:85:539:168:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:7For the same reasons, one of the first result that every graduate student learns is that the neoclassical
5:200:14288:16:8:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:growth model – the representative-agent counterpart to the Aiyagari model – features a unique steady state.
5:201:14395:8:16:85:539:144:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:8This is in contrast to discrete-time formulations where there is typically a critical level of wealth, strictly
5:202:14507:16:8:72:498:128:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:bigger than the borrowing constraint, such that the constraint binds for all lower levels of wealth.
5:203:14607:8:16:85:540:120:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:9Our numerical method is based on Achdou and Capuzzo-Dolcetta(2010) andAchdou(2013) but modified
5:204:14703:16:12:72:539:104:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:to handle the particular features of heterogeneous agent models, in particular borrowing constraints.Candler
5:205:14811:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:(1999) has previously used a finite difference method to solve HJB equations arising in economics and we
5:206:14915:12:0:72:272:80:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:discuss the relation to his work in Section3.7.
6:207:14963:0:16:72:539:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:general and applies toanyheterogeneous agent model with a continuum of atomistic agents
6:208:15050:16:17:71:539:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(and without aggregate shocks).  In Section 4 we demonstrate the algorithm’s generality by
6:209:15140:17:17:71:539:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:applying it to other theories that feature non-convexities, a fat-tailed wealth distribution and
6:210:15236:17:17:71:539:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:multiple assets.  Codes for these applications (and many more) are available fromhttp://
6:211:15324:17:20:71:539:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:www.princeton.edu/∼moll/HACTproject.htmin Matlab as well as Python, Julia and C++.
6:212:15406:20:16:89:539:633:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The  first  step  of  the  algorithm  is  to  solve  the  HJB  equation  for  a  given  time  path  of
6:213:15508:16:17:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:prices.  The second step is to solve the KF equation for the evolution of the joint distribution
6:214:15604:17:17:71:539:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of income and wealth.  Conveniently, after having solved the HJB equation, one obtains the
6:215:15694:17:17:71:539:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time path of the distribution essentially “for free,” i.e.  with very few lines of code.  This is
6:216:15791:17:16:71:539:566:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:because the KF equation is the “transpose problem” of the HJB equation.10The third step
6:217:15878:16:17:72:540:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is to iterate and repeat the first two steps until an equilibrium fixed point for the time path
6:218:15973:17:17:72:540:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of prices is found.  For the first step, we make use of the theory of “viscosity solutions” to
6:219:16067:17:17:72:539:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:HJB equations (Crandall and Lions,1983), and the corresponding theory for their numerical
6:220:16156:17:17:71:539:499:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solution using finite difference methods (Barles and Souganidis,1991).  While our paper can
6:221:16247:17:16:72:539:482:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:be read without knowledge of the theory of viscosity solutions, Online AppendixC.1provides
6:222:16337:16:20:72:534:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:an “economist-friendly” introduction and lists a number of relatively accessible references.
6:223:16429:20:17:89:539:446:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Continuous time imparts a number of computational advantages relative to discrete time.
6:224:16516:17:17:72:539:429:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:As explained in more detail in Section3.1, these relate to the handling of borrowing con-
6:225:16605:17:16:72:540:412:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:straints, the numerical solution of first-order conditions and the fact that continuous-time
6:226:16697:16:17:72:540:396:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:problems with discretized state space are, by construction, very “sparse.” These computa-
6:227:16786:17:17:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tional advantages are reflected in the algorithm’s efficiency.  Just to give a flavor, computing
6:228:16882:17:17:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the stationary equilibrium of Huggett’s economy with two income types and 1,000 grid points
6:229:16973:17:16:72:540:345:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in the wealth dimension using Matlab takes around a quarter second on a Macbook Pro lap-
6:230:17061:16:17:72:540:329:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:top computer.  At the same time,  the algorithm is simple.  Implementing it requires only
6:231:17150:17:17:72:540:312:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:some  basic  knowledge  of  matrix  algebra  and  access  to  a  software  package  that  can  solve
6:232:17250:17:17:72:540:295:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sparse  linear  systems  (e.g.   Matlab).   Finally,  the  algorithm  is  portable.   For  example,  it
6:233:17353:17:17:72:540:278:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:applies without change to problems that involve non-differentiabilities and non-convexities.
6:234:17445:17:16:72:540:261:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:These are difficult to handle with standard discrete-time methods.11In contrast, viscosity
6:235:17535:16:17:72:539:245:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solutions and finite difference methods aredesignedto handle non-differentiable and non-
6:236:17623:17:17:72:540:228:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:convex problems.  To illustrate this, we use the same algorithm to compute equilibria of an
6:237:17714:17:17:72:540:211:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:economy in which the interplay of indivisible housing and mortgages with a down-payment
6:238:17801:17:16:72:540:194:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:constraint cause a non-convexity which can result in individual poverty traps and multiple
6:239:17891:16:20:72:264:178:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stationary distributions (Section4.3).
6:240:17929:20:17:89:539:158:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Besides  hopefully  being  useful  in  their  own  right,  our  paper’s  contributions  also  con-
6:241:18027:17:18:72:540:141:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stitute  the  foundation  for  a  number  of  generalizations  that  go  beyond  the  setup  that  we
6:242:18128:18:15:81:539:123:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:10More precisely, the differential operator in the KF equation is the adjoint of the differential operator in
6:243:18237:15:9:72:530:108:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:the HJB equation.  The adjoint of an operator is the infinite-dimensional analogue of a matrix transpose.
6:244:18342:9:0:81:525:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:?:11Because first-order conditions are no longer sufficient and standard envelope theorems do not apply.
7:245:18445:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:consider  in  the  present  paper.   First,  building  on  the  finite  difference  method  developed
7:246:18546:16:17:72:539:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:here, Ahn, Kaplan, Moll, Winberry, and Wolf(2017) develop a computational method for
7:247:18630:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solving heterogeneous agent models with aggregate uncertainty in addition to idiosyncratic
7:248:18720:17:17:72:539:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:risk  (as  inDen  Haan,1997;Krusell  and  Smith,1998).   Second,Kaplan,  Moll,  and  Vi-
7:249:18808:17:17:72:540:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:olante(2016) incorporate multiple assets and kinked adjustment costs.  Third,Olivi(2017)
7:250:18896:17:16:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:leverages our continuous-time formulation to obtain powerful comparative statics and suf-
7:251:18985:16:17:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ficient  statistics  in  incomplete  markets  models  –  results  we  build  on  in  our  uniqueness
7:252:19085:17:17:72:539:603:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:proof.   Fourth,Parra-Alvarez,  Posch,  and  Wang(2017)  discuss  how  to  identify  and  esti-
7:253:19180:17:17:71:539:586:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:mate continuous-time Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett models.  Fifth,Nu ̃no and Moll(2017) and
7:254:19265:17:16:71:539:569:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Nu ̃no  and  Thomas(2017)  devise  a  method  for  computing  social  optima  in  such  environ-
7:255:19361:16:17:71:539:553:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ments.  Sixth,Shaker Akhtekhane(2017) extends our computational approach to economies
7:256:19446:17:17:71:540:536:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:with heterogeneous firms `a laHopenhayn(1992).  Seventh,Ahn(2017) computes equilibria
7:257:19531:17:17:71:539:519:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in economies with a large number of individual state variables (four or more) by marrying
7:258:19620:17:17:71:539:502:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:our finite difference method with a sparse grid approach (Bungartz and Griebel,2004;Ger-
7:259:19708:17:16:71:539:485:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stner and Griebel,2010).  All of these generalizations build on the tools developed in the
7:260:19798:16:20:71:144:469:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:present paper.
7:261:19812:20:17:89:539:449:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:A large theoretical and quantitative literature studies environments in which heteroge-
7:262:19899:17:17:71:539:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:neous households are subject to uninsurable idiosyncratic shocks.  SeeHeathcote, Storeslet-
7:263:19990:17:16:71:539:415:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ten,  and  Violante(2009),Guvenen(2011),Quadrini  and  R ́ıos-Rull(2015)  andKrueger,
7:264:20075:16:17:72:539:399:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Mitman, and Perri(2015) for recent surveys, and the textbook treatment inLjungqvist and
7:265:20162:17:20:71:338:382:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Sargent(2004).  All of these are set in discrete time.
7:266:20216:20:17:89:539:362:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Much fewer papers have studied equilibrium models with heterogeneous households in
7:267:20298:17:16:71:539:345:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:continuous time.12All of these papers make “just the right assumptions” about the envi-
7:268:20385:16:17:71:539:329:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ronment being studied so that equilibria can be solved explicitly (or at least characterized
7:269:20477:17:17:71:539:312:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tightly).13In contrast, our aim is to develop tools for solving and analyzing models that do
7:270:20569:17:17:71:539:295:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:not permit closed-form solutions.  Our methods apply as long as the model under consid-
7:271:20656:17:17:71:539:278:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:eration can be boiled down to an HJB equation and a KF equation, a feature shared by a
7:272:20742:17:16:71:539:261:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wide class of heterogeneous agent models.  These two approaches are clearly complimentary:
7:273:20832:16:17:71:539:245:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:on the one hand, having explicit solutions is often extremely valuable for gaining intuition;
7:274:20925:17:17:71:539:228:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:on the other hand, restricting attention to environments for which these can be found may
7:275:21014:17:18:71:539:211:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:represent a sort of “analytic straitjacket” for some applications and the availability of more
7:276:21108:18:15:81:539:193:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:12See for example Jovanovic (1979),  Moscarini(2005),Alvarez and Shimer(2011),Moll(2014),Stokey
7:277:21203:15:12:72:539:178:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:(2014),Vindigni, Scotti, and Tealdi(2014),Jones and Kim(2014),Jones(2015),Toda and Walsh(2015),
7:278:21298:12:12:72:539:166:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:Benhabib, Bisin, and Zhu(2016),Cao and Luo(2017) andKasa and Lei(2017).Luttmer(2007,2011,2015)
7:279:21392:12:12:71:539:154:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:andBenhabib, Perla, and Tonetti(2017) analyze theories with heterogeneous producers.  These papers, like
7:280:21496:12:12:71:539:142:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:ours, all study economies with a continuum of heterogeneous agents giving rise to a system of coupled HJB
7:281:21601:12:12:71:539:130:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:and  KF  equations.   In  contrast,  other  papers  study  environments  with  a  finite  number  of  heterogeneous
7:282:21716:12:12:71:539:118:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:agents (typically equal to two).  For example, seeScheinkman and Weiss(1986) and applications of their
7:283:21818:12:8:71:472:106:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:framework byConze, Lasry, and Scheinkman(1993) andLippi, Ragni, and Trachter(2013).
7:284:21901:8:16:81:539:98:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:13Similarly, there are also several discrete-time approaches for retaining tractability in environments with
7:285:22009:16:0:71:533:82:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:heterogeneous households (e.g.B ́enabou,2002;Krebs,2003;Heathcote, Storesletten, and Violante,2014).
8:286:22110:0:19:72:336:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:general methods may prove useful in such contexts.
8:287:22160:19:17:89:540:701:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:One other paper by Bayer and W ̈alde(2010) also studies a continuous-time version of
8:288:22244:17:17:71:539:684:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:an  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett  model.   The  main  differences  between  their  paper  and  ours
8:289:22338:17:17:71:539:667:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:are:  (i) they analyze a partial equilibrium framework whereas we consider a general equi-
8:290:22428:17:17:71:539:650:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:librium framework,  and (ii) we develop a numerical algorithm for solving both stationary
8:291:22517:17:16:71:539:633:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and  time-varying  equilibria,  thereby  actually  “operationalizing”  the  analysis  of  this  class
8:292:22618:16:17:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of models.14,15Also closely related,Rocheteau, Weill, and Wong(2015) propose an elegant
8:293:22705:17:17:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:alternative general equilibrium model with incomplete markets in continuous time.  Market
8:294:22794:17:17:72:540:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:incompleteness in their framework stems from lumpy consumption expenditure shocks (e.g.
8:295:22881:17:16:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:health events) rather than idiosyncratic income risk.  As a result their model features only
8:296:22973:16:17:72:540:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:one individual state variable and many results can be derived in closed form.  The tradeoff
8:297:23064:17:17:72:540:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is that their theory is further from the standard Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model that forms
8:298:23154:17:20:72:338:516:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:the backbone of much of modern macroeconomics.16
8:299:23202:20:16:89:540:496:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Section1 lays out our continuous-time version of the workhorse macroeconomic model
8:300:23284:16:17:72:539:480:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of income and wealth distribution in the parsimonious form due toHuggett(1993).  Section
8:301:23372:17:17:72:540:463:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:2 contains our new theoretical results.  Section3 describes our computational algorithm for
8:302:23463:17:17:72:540:446:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:both stationary and time-varying equilibria and discusses computational advantages relative
8:303:23554:17:17:72:539:429:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to  existing  discrete-time  methods.   Section4  demonstrates  the  algorithm’s  generality  by
8:304:23650:17:46:72:404:412:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:applying it to a number of other economies.  Section5 concludes.
8:305:23714:46:25:72:540:366:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:F:1    The Workhorse Model of Income and Wealth Distri-
8:306:23767:25:32:101:325:341:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:A:bution in Macroeconomics
8:307:23791:32:17:72:540:309:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:To  explain  the  logic  of  our  approach  in  the  simplest  possible  fashion,  we  present  it  in  a
8:308:23896:17:17:72:540:292:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:context that should be very familiar to many economists:  a general equilibrium model with
8:309:23986:17:16:72:539:275:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:incomplete  markets  and  uninsured  idiosyncratic  labor  income  risk  as  inAiyagari(1994),
8:310:24080:16:17:72:539:259:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Bewley(1986) andHuggett(1993).  We first do this in the context of an economy in which
8:311:24166:17:19:72:539:242:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:individuals save in unproductive bonds that are in fixed supply as inHuggett(1993).  We
8:312:24253:19:15:81:539:223:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:14Additionally, Bayer and W ̈alde study a version of the model with the “natural borrowing constraint”
8:313:24355:15:12:72:540:208:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:implying that  individuals never  actually hit  that  constraint.  Another difference  is that  they characterize
8:314:24468:12:12:72:540:196:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:individuals’  saving  behavior  in  terms  of  a  differential  equation  for  its  consumption  policy  function.   In
8:315:24587:12:12:72:540:184:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:contrast,  we  do  this  in  terms  of  the  HJB  equation  for  the  value  function,  thereby  allowing  us  to  take
8:316:24706:12:12:72:540:172:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:advantage of the theory of viscosity solutions.  Of course the two approaches are related and their equation
8:317:24814:12:8:72:429:160:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:for consumption can be derived from the envelope condition of the HJB equation.
8:318:24893:8:12:81:510:152:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:?:15Also Lise (2013) studies heterogeneous households in continuous time but in partial equilibrium.
8:319:24991:12:16:81:539:140:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:16Wang (2007) proposes an elegant continuous-time Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model that can be solved
8:320:25089:16:12:72:540:124:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:analytically but at the cost of making two non-standard assumptions on preferences:  that individuals have
8:321:25195:12:12:72:540:112:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:CARA  utility  and  that  current  discount  rates  are  increasing  in  past  consumption  (in  the  absence  of  the
8:322:25313:12:12:72:540:100:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:second assumption, CARA utility implies exploding wealth inequality and no stationary wealth distribution

13:490:34594:17:20:71:109:503:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to (11).
13:491:34602:20:17:89:539:483:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The two partial differential equations (12) and (13) together with (14), the equilibrium
13:492:34690:17:16:71:539:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:relationship (4) and the boundary conditions (15) to (17) fully characterize the evolution
13:493:34780:16:17:71:539:450:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of our economy.  This “Mean Field Game” has two properties that are worth emphasizing.
13:494:34866:17:17:71:539:433:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:First, the two equations (12) and (13) are coupled: on one hand, an individual’s consumption-
13:495:34959:17:17:71:539:416:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:saving decision depends on the evolution of the interest rate which is in turn determined by
13:496:35051:17:17:71:539:399:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the evolution of the distribution; on the other hand, the evolution of the distribution depends
13:497:35146:17:16:71:539:382:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:on  individuals’  saving  decisions.   Second,  the  two  equations  run  in  opposite  directions  in
13:498:35248:16:17:71:540:366:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f10:B:time:  the Kolmogorov Forward equationruns forward(as indicated by its name) andlooks
13:499:35333:17:17:71:539:349:12:g_d0_f10:12:g_d0_f6:B:backwards– it answers the question “given the wealth distribution today, savings decisions
13:500:35423:17:17:71:539:332:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and the random evolution of income, what is the wealth distribution tomorrow?” In contrast,
13:501:35514:17:16:71:539:315:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman equation (12)runs backwardsandlooks forward– it answers
13:502:35597:16:17:71:539:299:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the question “given an individual’s valuation of income and wealth tomorrow, how much will
13:503:35687:17:40:71:425:282:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:she save today and what is the corresponding value function today?”
13:504:35754:40:28:71:520:242:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:1.4    Classical versus Weak Solutions of HJB and KF equations
13:505:35816:28:16:71:539:214:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:A classical solution to a PDE or ODE is a solution that is differentiable as many times as
13:506:35906:16:17:71:539:198:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:needed to satisfy the corresponding equation.  In particular, classical solutions to the first-
13:507:36001:17:17:71:539:181:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:order HJB and KF equations (12) and (13) would need to be once differentiable.  Similarly,
13:508:36091:17:17:71:539:164:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:classical solutions to second-order equations that arise for example ifyfollows a diffusion
13:509:36182:17:16:71:539:147:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:process as in Section4.1would need to be twice differentiable.  In general, we do not expect
13:510:36274:16:17:71:539:131:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to  find  such  classical  solutions  to  either  HJB  or  KF  equations.   For  instance,  the  value
13:511:36376:17:17:71:540:114:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:functionvjmay have kinks and the distributiongjmay feature Dirac point masses.  Instead,
13:512:36464:17:17:71:539:97:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we generally look for certainweak  solutionsof these equations, that is solutions that may
13:513:36554:17:0:71:539:80:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:not be continuously differentiable or even continuous but still satisfy these equations in some
14:514:36651:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sense.  As we explain in more detail in Online AppendixC, the correct notion for a weak
14:515:36738:16:17:72:539:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f10:B:solution of the HJB equation is aviscosity solutionand that of the KF equation is ameasure-
14:516:36829:17:17:72:539:687:12:g_d0_f10:12:g_d0_f6:B:valued solution.25SeeEvans(2010, Section 1.3) andTao(2008) for illuminating discussions
14:517:36916:17:20:72:528:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:on the role of weak solutions in the study of partial differential equations more generally.
14:518:37008:20:17:89:539:650:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:That being said, most of our paper employs classical methods.  And, with the preceding
14:519:37094:17:46:72:456:633:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:paragraph in hand, the reader is well-equipped for those parts that do not.

15:551:39064:42:27:71:439:643:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:2.2    Consumption and Saving Behavior of the Poor
15:552:39114:27:17:71:539:616:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Our first main result is obtained by analyzing the Euler equation (18) close to the borrowing
15:553:39207:17:17:71:539:599:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:constraint.  The interesting case is when the behavior at the constraint differs qualitatively
15:554:39301:17:17:71:539:582:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:from  that  of  rich  individuals.   Whether  this  is  the  case  depends  crucially  on  two  factors:

21:837:52554:39:28:72:423:462:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:2.4    Marginal Propensities to Consume and Save

25:1049:61222:16:17:72:539:539:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:there is an offsetting effect captured by the term (ρ−r)c1/γ:  if consumption conditional

28:1230:66999:17:20:72:527:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the role of Assumption1 by making use of our two simple special cases from Section2.2.
28:1231:67085:20:17:89:539:667:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Part 2 of the Proposition states that the stationary wealth distribution in our economy
28:1232:67172:17:17:71:539:650:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is bounded above.  Like discrete-time versions of Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett economies with
28:1233:67260:17:16:71:539:633:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:idiosyncratic labor income risk only, our model therefore has difficulties explaining the high
28:1234:67354:16:17:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:observed wealth concentration in developed economies like the United States (e.g.  that the
28:1235:67445:17:17:71:539:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:top one percent of the population own roughly thirty-five percent of total wealth).  In par-
28:1236:67537:17:17:71:539:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ticular, the wealth distribution in the data appears to feature a fat Pareto tail.  Motivated
28:1237:67630:17:16:71:539:566:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:by this observation, we show in Section4.4how to extend the model to feature a station-
28:1238:67717:16:17:71:539:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ary distribution with a Pareto tail by introducing a second, risky asset.  This caveat aside,
28:1239:67810:17:17:71:539:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Proposition3 provides a complete characterization of the wealth distribution’s tail in the
28:1240:67900:17:17:71:539:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:vicinity of its upper bound.  From (40) top wealth inequality is high (gdeclines towards zero
28:1241:67993:17:17:71:539:499:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:atamaxonly slowly) if individuals face a high likelihood of dropping out of the high income
28:1242:68084:17:16:71:540:482:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:state (λ2is high) and if high-income types accumulate wealth only slowly (ζ2is low).  Intu-
28:1243:68175:16:17:71:539:466:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:itively, wealth accumulation requires both time and luck (consecutive high income draws).
28:1244:68264:17:17:71:539:449:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:And under the circumstances just mentioned, only a few individuals obtain sufficiently long
28:1245:68355:17:20:71:517:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:enough high income spells to accumulate large riches.  Hence, wealth inequality is high.
28:1246:68443:20:16:89:539:412:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Part 3, which can also be seen in Figure1, highlights an important difference between our
28:1247:68532:16:17:71:539:396:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:continuous-time formulation and the traditional discrete-time one: except for the Dirac mass
28:1248:68624:17:17:71:539:379:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:exactly at the borrowing constrainta, the wealth distribution is smooth for alla >a.  This is
28:1249:68717:17:17:72:540:362:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:true even though income follows a process with discrete states (a two-state Poisson process).
28:1250:68810:17:16:72:540:345:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:In  contrast,  the  wealth  distribution  in  discrete-time  versions  of  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett
28:1251:68908:16:17:72:540:329:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:models with discrete-state income processes, tends to feature “spikes” on the interior of the
28:1252:69001:17:20:72:472:312:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:state space.  See for example Figure 17.7.1 inLjungqvist and Sargent(2004).32
28:1253:69078:20:17:89:539:292:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Part  4  characterizes  the  wealth  distribution  for  intermediate  wealth  levels.   It  shows
28:1254:69175:17:17:72:540:275:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:that  the  shapes  ofg1andg2in  Figure6  are  not  simply  due  to  a  particular  numerical
28:1255:69267:17:16:71:539:258:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:example.   Instead  both  density  functions  are  simple  ratios  of  well-understood  functions
28:1256:69364:16:17:71:540:242:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:B:gj(a) =κjf(a)/sj(a) wherefis defined in the Proposition and hump-shaped.  For instance
28:1257:69450:17:17:71:539:225:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:considerg1asaincreases: as in Figure6,g1tends to be first decreasing, then increasing again

28:1260:69691:18:16:81:539:173:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:32To see why this must happen in discrete time, consider a discrete-time Huggett economy with two income
28:1261:69795:16:12:72:539:157:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:states.  All individuals with wealtha=awho get the high income draw choose the same wealth levela′>a.
28:1262:69896:12:12:72:538:145:10:g_d0_f9:7:g_d0_f19:F:So if there is a Dirac mass ata, there must also be a Dirac mass ata′.  But all individuals with wealtha′

28:1265:70216:11:12:72:540:110:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:into  the  rest  of  the  state  space.”  In  continuous  time  this  does  not  happen  because  individuals  leave  the
28:1266:70337:12:12:72:540:98:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:borrowing constraint in a smooth fashion (here according to a Poisson process, i.e.  a process with a random
28:1267:70445:12:0:72:258:86:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:and continuously distributed arrival time).

30:1350:73828:28:16:71:539:495:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:We construct stationary equilibria along the same lines as inAiyagari(1994).  That is, we fix

30:1354:74202:17:16:72:540:428:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:income types for the sake of continuity, all results in this section generalize to any stationary

33:1482:80162:17:20:72:141:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:more general.

33:1484:80263:16:17:72:540:480:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:that aggregate asset supply is increasing in the interest rate and hence uniqueness of the
33:1485:80353:17:41:72:242:463:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:stationary equilibrium (Part 4).38
33:1486:80387:41:27:72:466:422:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:2.7    Soft Borrowing Constraints and Non-Participation
33:1487:80442:27:17:72:540:395:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Empirical wealth distributions typically have the following properties:  there are individuals
33:1488:80536:17:17:72:540:378:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:with both positive and negative net worth but there is a spike at close to zero net worth.
33:1489:80626:17:17:72:540:361:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:This  empirical  observation  does  not  square  well  with the  Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model
33:1490:80720:17:16:72:539:344:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we have considered thus far.  If we set the borrowing constraint toa= 0, we get the spike
33:1491:80809:16:17:72:539:328:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:at zero but there are no individuals with negative net worth; if we seta <0, we get a spike
33:1492:80900:17:17:72:540:311:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:at a strictly negative wealth level.  Both are counterfactual.  A simple way of generating the
33:1493:80994:17:17:72:540:294:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:empirical observation just mentioned is to model a “soft” borrowing constraint as opposed
33:1494:81083:17:16:72:540:277:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to  the  “hard”  constraint  (3),  that  is,  a  wedge  between  borrowing  and  saving  rates.   This
33:1495:81185:16:17:72:539:261:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:form of constraint is used in a number of recent papers includingAlonso(2016) andKaplan,
33:1496:81273:17:17:72:539:244:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Moll, and Violante(2016).  In this section, we provide the first theoretical characterization
33:1497:81366:17:20:72:249:227:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of such soft borrowing constraints.
33:1498:81401:20:17:89:539:207:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Consider  the  Huggett  model  from  Section1  with  one  modification:  there  is  a  wedge
33:1499:81493:17:42:72:516:190:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:between borrowing and lending rates.  That is, we replace the budget constraint (2) by

33:1510:81843:12:0:71:521:85:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:model with an IES of 1/6.5, i.e.  considerably below one, as well as a somewhat special income process.
