35:1586:85050:17:17:72:539:401:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the right of zero it is necessary to introduce (at least) another income type.  Figure9(a) plots
35:1587:85146:17:16:72:539:384:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the saving policy functions in such a version with three income typesy0< y1< y2.  Figure
35:1588:85234:16:17:72:540:368:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:9(b) plots the resulting wealth densitiesg0,g1andg2.  The unconditional wealth distribution
35:1589:85325:17:17:72:540:351:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is the sum of these three densities.  As expected, it has a spike at zero and mass both to the
35:1590:85419:17:46:72:163:334:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:left and the right.
35:1591:85438:46:32:72:213:288:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:C:3    Computation
35:1592:85454:32:17:72:540:256:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:We now describe our algorithm for numerically computing equilibria of continuous-time het-
35:1593:85544:17:17:72:539:239:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:erogeneous agent models.  We use a finite difference method based on work byAchdou and
35:1594:85630:17:16:72:539:222:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Capuzzo-Dolcetta(2010) andAchdou(2013) which is simple, efficient and easily extended
35:1595:85715:16:17:72:540:206:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to other environments.  We explain our method in the context of the baseline heterogeneous
35:1596:85805:17:17:72:539:189:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:agent  model  of  Section1.   But  the  algorithm  is,  in  fact,  considerably  more  general  and
35:1597:85904:17:17:72:539:172:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:applies toanyheterogeneous agent model with a continuum of atomistic agents (and with-
35:1598:85990:17:16:72:540:155:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:out aggregate shocks).  It is particularly well-suited for computing transition dynamics and
35:1599:86082:16:17:72:540:139:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solving problems with non-convexities, a fact we illustrate in Section4 by computing equi-
35:1600:86172:17:17:72:540:122:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:libria of such economies.  Codes for these applications (and many more) are available from
35:1601:86262:17:17:72:539:105:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:http://www.princeton.edu/∼moll/HACTproject.htmin Matlab as well as Python, Julia and
35:1602:86346:17:0:72:102:88:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:C++.
36:1603:86352:0:27:72:483:720:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.1    Computational Advantages relative to Discrete Time
36:1604:86409:27:17:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Before explaining our algorithm, we provide a brief overview of some of its computational
36:1605:86498:17:17:72:540:676:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:advantages relative to traditional discrete-time methods.  We here list four computational
36:1606:86588:17:17:72:540:659:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:advantages that we consider crucial and that contribute notably to the efficiency gains over
36:1607:86680:17:16:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:traditional methods.  The first of these advantages is special to the solution of problems with
36:1608:86775:16:17:72:540:626:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:borrowing constraints.  The second to fourth advantages concern the solution of heteroge-
36:1609:86864:17:20:72:450:609:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:neous agent models more broadly (e.g.  models with heterogeneous firms).
36:1610:86936:20:17:89:541:589:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:To appreciate the first two advantages, contrast the first-order condition of the continuous-
36:1611:87029:17:16:72:540:572:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time income fluctuation problem (7) with that in the analogous discrete-time problem.  For
36:1612:87119:16:31:72:449:556:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:concreteness also assume CRRA utility (5) so that the two conditions are
36:1613:87191:31:30:161:539:525:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:c−γ=v′j(a),  j= 1,2in continuous time and(46)
36:1614:87236:30:0:161:201:495:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f12:A:c−γ≥β
36:1615:87241:0:29:210:221:510:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f20:A:2∑
36:1616:87243:29:0:204:220:481:8:g_d0_f14:8:g_d0_f7:A:k=1
36:1617:87246:0:39:223:539:495:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:πjkv′k(a′),  a′=yj+ (1 +r)a−c,  j= 1,2(47)
36:1618:87288:39:17:72:539:456:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in discrete time,  where 0< β <1 is a discount factor andπjk= Pr(y′=yk|y=yj) are
36:1619:87368:17:17:72:540:439:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the entries of the Markov transition matrix for the analogous discrete-time income process.
36:1620:87459:17:17:72:539:422:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The first advantage of our continuous-time approach is that, as explained in Section1.2, the
36:1621:87551:17:17:72:540:405:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:borrowing constraint (3) only shows up in the boundary condition (10) and therefore the
36:1622:87638:17:16:72:539:388:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:first-order condition (46) holdswith  equalityeverywhere in the interior of the state space.
36:1623:87730:16:17:72:540:372:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:In contrast, the discrete-time first-order condition (47) holds with complementary slackness
36:1624:87822:17:17:72:540:355:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and therefore is aninequality.  This is because the borrowing constraint may bind one time
36:1625:87912:17:17:72:540:338:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:period  ahead.   Continuous  time  allows  us  to  completely  sidestep  any  technical  difficulties
36:1626:88013:17:19:72:339:321:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:arising due to such occasionally binding constraints.
36:1627:88066:19:17:89:539:302:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Second and related, the first-order condition in (46) is “static” in the sense that it only
36:1628:88157:17:17:72:539:285:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:involves  contemporaneous  variables.   Given  (a  guess  for)  the  value  functionvj(a)  it  can
36:1629:88255:17:17:72:539:268:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:be  solved  by  hand:cj(a)  =  (v′j(a))−1/γ,j=  1,2.   In  contrast,  the  discrete-time  condition
36:1630:88354:17:16:72:539:251:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(47) defines the optimal choiceonly  implicitly.  Typical solution methods therefore employ
36:1631:88445:16:17:72:538:235:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:costly root-finding operations. Our continuous-time approach again sidesteps this difficulty.40
36:1632:88540:17:17:72:540:218:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Intuitively, discrete time distinguishes between “today” and “tomorrow” but in continuous
36:1633:88629:17:20:72:230:201:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:time, “tomorrow”is“today.”41
36:1634:88657:20:17:89:539:181:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The third advantage of continuous time is a form of “sparsity.” To solve the HJB and
36:1635:88741:17:18:72:540:164:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:KF equations (7) and (8), we discretize these so that their solution boils down to solving
36:1636:88831:18:16:81:539:146:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:40In this regard,  it shares some similarities with the “endogenous grid method” ofCarroll(2006).  The
36:1637:88933:16:8:72:404:130:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:difference is that in continuous time this also works with “exogenous grids.”
36:1638:89010:8:16:81:539:122:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:41Related, (46) also does not involve an expectation operator as in (47) that makes it necessary to calculate
36:1639:89119:16:11:72:540:106:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:a summation over future income states or a costly numerical integral (in the context of more general income
36:1640:89226:11:12:72:539:95:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:processes).   Instead  the  HJB  equation  (7)  captures  the  evolution  of  the  stochastic  process  forytwith  an
36:1641:89343:12:0:72:417:83:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:additive termsλj(v−j(a)−vj(a)) that does not affect the first-order condition.
37:1642:89423:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:systems of linear equations.  The resulting matrices are typically extremely sparse, namely
37:1643:89514:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:“tridiagonal” or at least “block-tridiagonal.” This sparsity generates considerable efficiency
37:1644:89608:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:gains  because  there  are  well-developed  routines  for  solving  sparse  linear  systems,  either
37:1645:89708:17:17:72:540:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:implemented as part of commercial software packages like Matlab or open-source libraries
37:1646:89796:17:17:72:540:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:like SuiteSparse.  The reason that tridiagonal matrices arise is that a discretized continuous-
37:1647:89891:17:16:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time process either stays at the current grid point, takes one step to the left or one step to
37:1648:89985:16:20:72:233:620:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:the right.  But it never jumps.42
37:1649:90018:20:17:89:539:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Fourth, in all heterogeneous agent models, there is a tight link between solving the HJB
37:1650:90106:17:17:72:540:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and  KF  equations.   One  can  typically  “kill  two  birds  with  one  stone”  in  the  sense  that,
37:1651:90208:17:16:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:having computed the solution to the HJB equation one gets the solution to the KF equation
37:1652:90297:16:17:72:540:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:“for free”:  the matrix in the discretized version of the latter is the transpose of the matrix
37:1653:90392:17:17:72:540:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in that of the former.  The underlying mathematical reason is that the KF equation is the
37:1654:90481:17:17:72:540:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:“transpose problem” of the HJB equation or, more precisely, that the differential operator
37:1655:90571:17:40:72:442:499:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:in the KF equation is the adjoint of the operator in the HJB equation.43
37:1656:90643:40:27:72:499:459:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.2    Bird’s Eye View of Algorithm for Stationary Equilibria
37:1657:90704:27:17:72:539:432:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:B:Our  aim  is  to  calculate  stationary  equilibria  –  functionsv1,v2andg1,g2and  a  scalarr
37:1658:90797:17:17:72:539:415:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:satisfying  (7),  (8)  and  (11)  –  given  a  specified  functionu,  and  values  for  the  parameters
37:1659:90900:17:16:72:540:398:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:B:ρ,λ1,λ2anda.  Transition dynamics are the subject of Section3.5.  Before we describe the
37:1660:90988:16:17:72:540:382:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:algorithm in detail, we provide a bird’s eye view of the algorithm’s general structure.  We
37:1661:91079:17:17:72:540:365:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:focus on two distinct challenges.  First, the HJB and KF equations describing a stationary
37:1662:91169:17:17:72:540:348:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equilibrium are coupled and one therefore has to iterate on them somehow.  Second, solving
37:1663:91259:17:35:72:517:331:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:these differential equations requires approximating the value function and distribution.
37:1664:91347:35:17:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:Iterating on the Equilibrium SystemFrom a bird’s eye perspective our algorithm for
37:1665:91429:17:17:72:540:279:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solving the stationary equilibrium shares many similarities with algorithms typically used
37:1666:91519:17:17:72:540:262:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to solve discrete-time heterogeneous agent models.  In the context of our Huggett economy,
37:1667:91609:17:17:72:540:245:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we use a bisection algorithm on the stationary interest rate.  We begin an iteration with an
37:1668:91701:17:25:72:318:228:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:initial guessr0.  Then for`= 0,1,2,...we follow
37:1669:91748:25:17:86:540:203:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:1.  Givenr`, solve the HJB equation (7) using a finite difference method.  Calculate the
37:1670:91836:17:18:101:246:186:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:saving policy functions`j(a).
37:1671:91865:18:16:81:539:168:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:42Except of course if the process is a Poisson process, i.e.  if jumps are “built in.” That being said, the
37:1672:91972:16:12:72:540:152:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:sparsity property survives as long as there is at least one continuously moving state variable (like wealth),
37:1673:92081:12:8:72:402:140:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:i.e.  not all individual state variables follow discrete-state Poisson processes.
37:1674:92162:8:16:81:539:132:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:43In principle, one can use an analogous approach in discrete time: form the endogenous Markov transition
37:1675:92267:16:12:72:540:116:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:matrix between states and use it both to iterate backward over value functions (or Euler equations) and to
37:1676:92373:12:12:72:540:104:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:iterate forward over distributions.  This method does not appear to be very popular and researchers typically
37:1677:92482:12:12:72:540:92:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:use Monte-Carlo simulation to solve for the distribution.  A possible reason is that transition matrices are
37:1678:92590:12:0:72:343:80:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:not (or less) sparse resulting in a less dramatic efficiency gain.
38:1679:92658:0:26:86:515:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:2.  Givens`j(a), solve the KF equation (8) forg`j(a) using a finite difference method.
38:1680:92744:26:0:86:395:694:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:3.  Giveng`j(a),  compute  the  net  supply  of  bondsS(r`)  =
38:1681:92806:0:14:401:417:703:12:g_d0_f16:8:g_d0_f13:A: ́∞
38:1682:92809:14:0:406:436:689:8:g_d0_f14:12:g_d0_f12:?:aa(g
38:1683:92813:0:7:436:439:698:8:g_d0_f14:8:g_d0_f14:?:`
38:1684:92814:7:0:436:478:691:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f12:?:1(a) +g
38:1685:92821:0:7:479:482:698:8:g_d0_f14:8:g_d0_f14:?:`
38:1686:92822:7:14:478:539:691:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:A:2(a))daand
38:1687:92832:14:30:101:498:677:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:update the interest rate:  ifS(r`)> B, decrease it tor`+1< r`and vice versa.
38:1688:92908:30:17:72:539:647:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Whenr`+1is  close  enough  tor`,  we  call  (r`,v`1,v`2,g`1,g`2)  a  stationary  equilibrium.   As
38:1689:93006:17:16:72:540:630:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:already noted, this algorithm is extremely close to typical algorithms used to solve a discrete-
38:1690:93102:16:17:72:540:614:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:time Huggett economy.  The difference of our continuous-time approach – and the resulting
38:1691:93191:17:17:72:540:597:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:efficiency gains – instead lie in the solutions of the dynamic programming equation and the
38:1692:93282:17:37:72:258:580:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equation describing the distribution.
38:1693:93319:37:16:72:539:543:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:Discretization of the Equilibrium SystemIn order to solve the differential equations
38:1694:93403:16:17:72:540:527:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(7) and (8), the value function and distribution need to be approximated in some fashion.
38:1695:93492:17:17:72:540:510:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:We  explain  our  approach  –  a  finite  difference  method  –  in  more  detail  in  the  next  two
38:1696:93593:17:17:72:540:493:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:subsections.  But a brief sketch is as follows.  In a nutshell, the key idea is that this finite
38:1697:93689:17:16:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:difference  method  transforms  our  system  of  differential  equations  into  a  system  of  sparse
38:1698:93790:16:17:72:539:460:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:matrix equations.  With this goal in mind, we approximate bothv1,v2andg1,g2atIdiscrete
38:1699:93876:17:17:72:540:443:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:points in the space dimension,ai,i= 1,...,I.  Denote the value function and distribution
38:1700:93964:17:17:72:539:426:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:along this discrete grid using the vectorsv= (v1(a1),...,v1(aI),v2(a1),...,v2(aI))Tandg=
38:1701:94052:17:16:72:539:409:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(g1(a1),...,g1(aI),g2(a1),...,g2(aI))T; bothvandgare of dimension 2I×1, the total number
38:1702:94140:16:17:72:540:393:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of grid points in the individual state space.  The end product of our discretization method
38:1703:94231:17:31:72:348:376:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:will then be the following system of matrix equations:
38:1704:94285:31:20:248:539:345:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:ρv=u(v) +A(v;r)v,(48)
38:1705:94306:20:19:254:539:325:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:A:0=A(v;r)Tg,(49)
38:1706:94321:19:31:252:539:306:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:B=S(g;r).(50)
38:1707:94334:31:17:72:540:275:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The first equation is the discretized HJB equation (7), the second equation is the discretized
38:1708:94428:17:17:72:539:258:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:KF equation (8) and the third equation is the discretized market clearing condition (11).
38:1709:94517:17:16:72:539:241:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The 2I×2ImatrixA(v;r) will have the interpretation of a transition matrix that captures
38:1710:94604:16:17:72:540:225:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the evolution of the idiosyncratic state variables in the discretized state space.  It will turn
38:1711:94700:17:17:72:539:208:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:out to be extremely sparse.A(v;r)Tin the second equation denotes the transpose of that
38:1712:94786:17:17:72:540:191:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:same matrix, i.e.  the discretized KF equation is the “transpose problem” of the discretized
38:1713:94878:17:16:72:539:174:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:HJB equation.  As already noted, (48) to (50) is simply a system of sparse matrix equations
38:1714:94969:16:17:72:540:158:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:that can be easily solved on a computer by following (the analogues of) Steps 1 to 3 described
38:1715:95063:17:0:72:208:141:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in the previous paragraph.
39:1716:95091:0:27:72:352:720:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.3    Step 1:  Solving the HJB Equation
39:1717:95131:27:17:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:For step 1, we solve the HJB equation (7) using a finite difference method.  We now explain
39:1718:95222:17:36:72:457:676:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:this approach.  The Online Appendix contains a more detailed explanation.
39:1719:95295:36:17:72:540:640:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:Theory  for  Numerical  Solution  of  HJB  Equations  (Barles-Souganidis)Before
39:1720:95374:17:17:72:540:623:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we explain our approach we should note that there is a well-developed theory concerning
39:1721:95461:17:17:72:540:606:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the numerical solution of HJB equations using finite difference schemes in the same way as
39:1722:95551:17:17:72:540:589:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:there is a well-developed theory concerning the numerical solution of discrete-time Bellman
39:1723:95642:17:16:72:539:572:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equations.   The  key  result  is  due  to  Barles  and  Souganidis(1991)  who  have  proven  that,
39:1724:95741:16:17:72:540:556:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:under certain conditions, the solution to a finite difference scheme converges to the (unique
39:1725:95834:17:17:72:539:539:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:viscosity) solution of the HJB equation.44The interested reader should consult Barles and
39:1726:95923:17:17:72:539:522:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Souganidis’ original (and relatively accessible) paper or the introduction byTourin(2013). In
39:1727:96016:17:16:72:540:505:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:short, for their result to hold, the finite difference scheme needs to satisfy three conditions: (i)
39:1728:96116:16:17:72:540:489:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:“monotonicity”, (ii) “stability” and (iii) “consistency.” These are spelled out in the Online
39:1729:96209:17:17:72:540:472:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Appendix.   Here  it  suffices  to  note  that  (ii)  and  (iii)  are  typically  easy  to  satisfy  and,  in
39:1730:96318:17:17:72:540:455:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:practice, the main difficulty for the numerical solution of HJB equations is to design a finite
39:1731:96413:17:36:72:401:438:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:difference scheme that satisfies (iii), the monotonicity condition.
39:1732:96480:36:17:72:539:402:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:Finite Difference MethodWe here explain the finite difference method for solving the
39:1733:96564:17:17:72:540:385:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stationary HJB equation for a special case we have already examined earlier in the paper,
39:1734:96653:17:17:72:540:368:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:namely the one with no income uncertaintyy1=y2=y.  The generalization to income risk
39:1735:96737:17:30:72:377:351:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is straightforward.  The HJB equation in this special case is
39:1736:96798:30:7:208:273:321:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:ρv(a) = max
39:1737:96809:7:0:260:264:314:8:g_d0_f14:8:g_d0_f14:A:c
39:1738:96810:0:31:279:539:321:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:u(c) +v′(a)(y+ra−c).(51)
39:1739:96834:31:17:72:539:290:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:As already mentioned, the finite difference method approximates the functionvatIdiscrete
39:1740:96922:17:16:72:539:273:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:points in the space dimension,ai,i= 1,...,I.  We use equispaced grids, denote by ∆athe
39:1741:97008:16:17:72:539:257:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:distance between grid points, and use the short-hand notationvi:=v(ai).  The derivative
39:1742:97095:17:33:72:529:240:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:B:v′i=v′(ai) is approximated with either a forward or a backward difference approximation
39:1743:97182:33:0:149:189:207:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f11:?:v′(ai)≈
39:1744:97189:0:16:193:236:215:12:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f14:?:vi+1−vi
39:1745:97196:16:0:207:223:199:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:A:∆a
39:1746:97198:0:0:241:372:207:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f11:?:=:v′i,Forv′(ai)≈
39:1747:97214:0:16:377:420:215:12:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f7:A:vi−vi−1
39:1748:97221:16:0:391:406:199:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:A:∆a
39:1749:97223:0:31:425:462:207:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:A:=:v′i,B.
39:1750:97231:31:31:72:330:176:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The finite difference approximation to (51) is then
39:1751:97282:31:19:131:539:145:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:ρvi=u(ci) +v′isi,  si:=y+rai−ci,  ci= (u′)−1(v′i),  i= 1,...,I(52)
39:1752:97348:19:0:81:503:126:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:?:44See Appendix C.1for an “economist-friendly” introduction to the theory of viscosity solutions.
40:1753:97446:0:16:72:539:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wherev′iis  either  the  forward  or  backward  difference  approximation.   Which  of  the  two
40:1754:97542:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:approximations is used and where in the state space is extremely important.  The reason
40:1755:97629:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is  that  this  choice  determines  whether  Barles  and  Souganidis’  monotonicity  condition  is
40:1756:97727:17:36:72:116:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:satisfied.
40:1757:97737:36:17:72:539:634:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:UpwindingAs just mentioned, it is important whether and when a forward or a backward
40:1758:97821:17:17:71:539:617:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:difference  approximation is  used.  The ideal solution  to this problem  is to use  a so-called
40:1759:97917:17:17:71:539:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:“upwind scheme.” The rough idea is to use a forward difference approximation whenever the
40:1760:98006:17:16:71:539:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:drift of the state variable (here, savingsi=y+rai−ci) is positive and to use a backward
40:1761:98093:16:17:71:539:567:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:difference whenever it is negative.  This is intuitive:  if saving is positive,  what matters is
40:1762:98189:17:17:71:539:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:how the value function changes when wealth increases by a small amount;  and vice versa
40:1763:98276:17:17:71:539:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:when saving is negative.  The right thing to do is therefore to approximate the derivative in
40:1764:98369:17:16:71:539:516:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the direction of the movement of the state.  To this end use the notations+i= max{si,0},
40:1765:98457:16:17:71:539:500:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:i.e.s+iis “the positive part ofsi” and analogouslys−i= min{si,0}.  The upwind version of
40:1766:98545:17:19:71:130:483:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(52) is then
40:1767:98557:19:0:163:229:464:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:ρvi=u(ci) +
40:1768:98568:0:16:234:277:472:12:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f14:?:vi+1−vi
40:1769:98575:16:0:247:263:456:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:A:∆a
40:1770:98577:0:0:278:303:464:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:s+i+
40:1771:98581:0:16:307:349:472:12:g_d0_f12:8:g_d0_f7:A:vi−vi−1
40:1772:98588:16:0:320:336:456:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:?:∆a
40:1773:98590:0:26:351:539:464:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:s−i,   i= 1,...,I,(53)
40:1774:98612:26:17:71:539:438:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:where also the finite difference approximationv′iused to computeci= (u′)−1(v′i) depends on
40:1775:98702:17:17:71:539:421:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the sign ofsiin the same way.  This simplified exposition ignores two important and related
40:1776:98793:17:17:71:539:404:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:issues.  First, that the HJB equation (51) is highly non-linear due to the presence of the max
40:1777:98887:17:17:71:539:387:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:operator, and therefore so is its finite difference approximation (53).  It therefore has to be
40:1778:98982:17:16:71:539:370:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solved using an iterative scheme and one faces a choice between using so-called “explicit”
40:1779:99072:16:17:71:539:354:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f14:B:and  “implicit”  schemes.   Related,  from  the  first-order  conditionci=  (u′)−1(v′i),  savingsi
40:1780:99170:17:17:72:539:337:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and consumptioncithemselves depend on whether the forward or backward approximation
40:1781:99253:17:17:72:540:320:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is used so (53) has a circular element to it.  The solution to both these issues is described in
40:1782:99349:17:19:72:229:303:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:detail in the Online Appendix.
40:1783:99379:19:17:89:539:284:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The upwind finite difference scheme for the HJB equation (53) can be conveniently written
40:1784:99468:17:17:72:539:267:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in matrix notation.  Denoting byv= (v1,...,vI)Tthe vector collecting the value function at
40:1785:99558:17:17:72:539:250:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:different grid points, we have the matrix equation (48).  The matrixA(v;r) has a special
40:1786:99646:17:16:72:540:233:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:structure:   first,  it  is  sparse;  more  precisely,  it  is  tridiagonal:   all  entries  are  zero  except
40:1787:99756:16:17:72:540:217:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:for those on the main diagonal, the first diagonal below this, and the first diagonal above
40:1788:99847:17:0:72:475:200:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f14:B:the  main  diagonal.   Second,  all  diagonal  entries  are  negative  and  given  bys
40:1789:99933:0:7:475:481:210:6:g_d0_f55:6:g_d0_f55:?:−
40:1790:99934:7:7:475:478:203:6:g_d0_f54:6:g_d0_f54:?:i
40:1791:99935:7:0:470:495:196:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f11:A:∆a−
40:1792:99938:0:10:501:508:206:8:g_d0_f14:6:g_d0_f54:A:s+i
40:1793:99941:10:15:500:539:196:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:A:∆a≤0
40:1794:99945:15:0:72:372:181:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f14:B:and  all  off-diagonal  entries  are  positive  and  given  by−s
40:1795:100009:0:14:371:374:191:6:g_d0_f55:6:g_d0_f54:?:−i
40:1796:100011:14:0:367:430:177:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:?:∆a≥0  and
40:1797:100020:0:10:438:444:187:8:g_d0_f14:6:g_d0_f54:?:s+i
40:1798:100023:10:13:437:539:177:8:g_d0_f7:12:g_d0_f6:A:∆a≥0.   Third,  all
40:1799:100042:13:17:72:539:164:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:rows  ofA(v;r)  sum  to  zero.   All  these  properties  are  extremely  intuitive.   In  effect,  the
40:1800:100144:17:16:72:540:147:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:finite difference method approximates the law of motion for the continuous state variable
40:1801:100233:16:17:72:540:131:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:B:awith  a  discrete-state  Poisson  process  on  the  gridai,i=  1,...,Iand  the  matrixA(v;r)
40:1802:100326:17:17:72:540:114:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:summarizes the corresponding Poisson intensities.  The properties noted above are precisely
40:1803:100417:17:17:72:540:97:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the properties that a Poisson transition matrix needs to satisfy.  For these reasons we will
40:1804:100509:17:0:72:539:80:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sometimes refer toA(v;r) as “Poisson transition matrix” or “intensity matrix.” All this will
41:1805:100603:0:36:72:411:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:be useful in Section3.4below when we solve the KF equation (8).
41:1806:100666:36:17:72:539:684:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f6:B:Boundary  Conditions  and  Handling  the  Borrowing  ConstraintBesides  guaran-
41:1807:100745:17:17:72:540:667:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:teeing that the Barles-Souganidis monotonicity condition holds, an upwind scheme like (53)
41:1808:100835:17:17:72:540:650:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:has an additional great advantage:  the handling of boundary conditions.  First, consider the
41:1809:100928:17:16:72:539:633:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:upper end of the state spaceaI.  If this upper end is large enough, saving will be negative
41:1810:101019:16:17:72:539:617:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:B:sI<0 so thats+I= 0.45It can then be seen from (53) that the forward difference is never
41:1811:101106:17:17:72:540:600:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:used at the upper end of the state space.  As a result no boundary condition needs to be
41:1812:101194:17:17:72:540:583:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:imposed.  Next, consider the lower end of the state space and in particular the question how
41:1813:101286:17:16:72:540:566:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to impose the state constraint boundary condition (10) which holds with equality only when
41:1814:101376:16:17:72:540:550:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the  constraint  binds.   To  impose  this,  we  can  exploit  the  special  structure  of  the  upwind
41:1815:101479:17:31:72:255:533:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:scheme:  set the boundary condition
41:1816:101514:31:30:260:351:502:12:g_d0_f12:12:g_d0_f6:A:v′1,B=u′(y+ra1)
41:1817:101529:30:17:72:540:472:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:but only for the backward difference approximation and not for the forward difference ap-
41:1818:101618:17:17:72:539:455:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:proximationv′1,Fwhich is instead computed as (v2−v1)/∆a.  Then let the upwind scheme
41:1819:101702:17:17:72:540:438:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:itself select whether this boundary condition is used.  From (53) we can see that the bound-
41:1820:101794:17:17:72:539:421:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ary condition is only imposed if it would be the case thats1<0; but it is not used ifs1>0.
41:1821:101884:17:40:72:383:404:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:This ensures that the borrowing constraint is never violated.
41:1822:101945:40:28:72:468:364:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.4    Step 2:  Solving the Kolmogorov Forward Equation
41:1823:102000:28:16:72:540:336:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:For step 2, consider the stationary Kolmogorov Forward equation (8).  We again discretize
41:1824:102089:16:17:72:540:320:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the  equation  using  a  finite  difference  scheme.   In  contrast  to  the  HJB  equation  which  is
41:1825:102191:17:17:72:539:303:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:non-linear  inv,  the  KF  equation  is  linear  ing.   Its  discretized  counterpart  can  therefore
41:1826:102292:17:17:72:540:286:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:be  solved  in  one  iteration.   There  are  a  number  of  potentially  admissible  finite  difference
41:1827:102396:17:16:72:540:269:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:schemes,  but  one  of  these  is  particularly  convenient  and  deeply  rooted  in  mathematical
41:1828:102494:16:17:72:539:253:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:theory:  the discretization (49) which involves thetransposeofA(v,r), the transition matrix
41:1829:102585:17:20:72:337:236:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of the discretized stochastic process in (a,yj)-space.
41:1830:102639:20:17:89:539:216:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The  deep  underlying  reason  for  this  choice  of  discretization  is  that  the  KF  equation
41:1831:102736:17:16:72:539:199:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:actuallyisthe “transpose” problem of the HJB equation.  More precisely,  the differential
41:1832:102825:16:17:72:539:183:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:operator  in  the  KF  equation  (8)  is  the  adjoint  of  the  operator  in  the  HJB  equation  (7),
41:1833:102928:17:19:72:539:166:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the “infinitesimal generator.”46Our transpose discretization of the KF equation (49) is not
41:1834:103019:19:15:81:539:147:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:45In fact, in the special case without uncertainty analyzed in the present section and under the assumption
41:1835:103126:15:12:72:539:132:10:g_d0_f17:10:g_d0_f9:F:r < ρ,  saving is negative everywhere in the state space.  The condition thataIneeds to be large enough
41:1836:103229:12:12:72:540:120:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:really only matters for the case with uncertainty.  That the case without uncertainty is special also applies
41:1837:103338:12:9:72:520:108:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:to the subsequent discussion:  without uncertainty the state constraint (10) always holds with equality.
41:1838:103442:9:15:81:539:99:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:46The “infinitesimal generator” is the continuous-time analogue of a discrete-time transition matrix, and
41:1839:103547:15:0:72:540:84:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:the adjoint of an operator is the infinite-dimensional analogue of a matrix transpose.  In our context, the
42:1840:103656:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:only well-founded in mathematics; it is also extremely convenient:  having solved the HJB
42:1841:103745:16:20:72:500:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equation, the solution of the Kolmogorov Forward equation is essentially “for free.”
42:1842:103829:20:17:89:539:684:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The same numerical method – building the matrixAand then working with its transpose
42:1843:103912:17:17:72:539:667:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:– can also be used when solving problems that involveonlythe KF equation, e.g.  because
42:1844:103999:17:17:72:540:650:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the  optimal  decision  rules  can  be  solved  for  analytically.   This  approach  is,  for  example,
42:1845:104102:17:19:72:476:633:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:pursued in Jones and Kim(2014) andGabaix, Lasry, Lions, and Moll(2016).47
42:1846:104175:19:17:89:539:614:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Finally, some readers may be concerned that the presence of a Dirac point mass in the
42:1847:104260:17:17:71:539:597:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stationary wealth distributiong1may cause problems for our finite difference method.  We
42:1848:104348:17:17:71:539:580:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:show in AppendixDthat this is not the case.  First,  we show theoretically that the only
42:1849:104436:17:16:71:539:563:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:implication of the Dirac mass is that some care is required wheninterpretingthe output of
42:1850:104525:16:17:72:539:547:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the numerical algorithm,  in particular the first element of the vectorg(corresponding to
42:1851:104614:17:17:72:539:530:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the density of income typey1at the pointa=a).  Second, we use the analytic solution for
42:1852:104701:17:17:72:539:513:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the wealth distribution in Proposition3 as a test case for our numerical algorithm and show
42:1853:104792:17:40:72:482:496:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:that it performs extremely well in practice unless the wealth grid is very coarse.
42:1854:104874:40:28:72:340:456:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.5    Computing Transition Dynamics
42:1855:104910:28:17:72:539:428:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f12:B:The  algorithm  we  use  to  calculate  time-varying  equilibria  –  functionsv1,v2,g1,g2andr
42:1856:105003:17:16:72:540:411:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:satisfying (4), (12) and (13) given an initial condition (16) and a terminal condition (17) –
42:1857:105096:16:17:72:540:395:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is the natural generalization of that used to compute stationary equilibria.  We again use a

43:1896:106416:0:19:72:511:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Whenr`+1(t) is close enough tor`(t) for allt, we call (r`,v`1,v`2,g`1,g`2) an equilibrium.
43:1897:106506:19:17:89:539:701:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:The finite difference method used for computing the time-dependent HJB and KF equa-
43:1898:106589:17:17:72:540:684:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tions for a given time pathr`(t) is similar to those used to compute their stationary coun-

43:1912:107176:17:17:72:539:480:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solution to (48) and with initial conditiong1=g0.  All this is explained in more detail in
43:1913:107266:17:40:72:183:463:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the Online Appendix.
43:1914:107286:40:28:72:492:423:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:3.6    The Method’s Performance:  Some Illustrative Results
43:1915:107345:28:16:72:539:395:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:All of Figures1,6 and8 for the Huggett economy in Section1 earlier in the paper were
43:1916:107429:16:17:72:540:379:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:computed using a Matlab implementation of the algorithm just laid out.  Even though we
43:1917:107515:17:17:72:539:362:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:work with a fine wealth grid withI= 1000 grid points, solving for a stationary equilibrium
43:1918:107605:17:17:72:540:345:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:takes  about  0.25  seconds  on  a  MacBook  Pro  laptop  computer.   Next,  consider  the  corre-
43:1919:107703:17:17:72:539:328:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sponding transition dynamics.  WithI= 1000 wealth grid points,N= 400 time steps and
43:1920:107786:17:16:72:539:311:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the same hardware, computing (12) and (13) for a fixed time pathr(t) takes about 2 seconds.
43:1921:107877:16:17:72:539:295:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Iterating onr(t) until an equilibrium transition is found takes about 4 minutes (even though
43:1922:107969:17:17:72:540:278:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:market clearing conditions like (4) that implicitly define prices are notoriously difficult to
43:1923:108063:17:18:72:218:261:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:impose during transitions).48
43:1924:108092:18:16:81:540:243:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:48In  contrast,  computing  transitions  for  the  Aiyagari  model  in  Section4.2,  where  prices  are  explicit
43:1925:108205:16:12:71:539:227:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:functions  of  the  aggregate  capital  stock  as  in  (61),  takes  only  1  minute  and  40  seconds.The  code
43:1926:108317:12:12:71:539:215:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:for  the  stationary  equilibrium  and  transition  dynamics  of  the  Huggett  model  are  available  athttp:
43:1927:108427:12:10:72:540:203:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F://www.princeton.edu/∼moll/HACTproject/huggettequilibriumiterate.m  and  http://www.princeton.edu/
43:1928:108524:10:14:72:539:193:7:g_d0_f19:10:g_d0_f9:F:∼moll/HACTproject/huggetttransition.m.  The code for the Aiyagari model is at http://www.princeton.
43:1929:108623:14:0:72:318:179:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:edu/∼moll/HACTproject/aiyagaripoissonMITshock.m.
44:1930:108673:0:21:72:540:720:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:F:3.7    Relation to Candler (1999) and Alternatives to Finite Differ-
44:1931:108741:21:27:108:200:699:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:ence Method
44:1932:108752:27:17:72:540:672:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Candler (1999) has previously used a finite difference method to solve HJB equations arising
44:1933:108844:17:17:72:540:655:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in economics (with the neoclassical growth model as his prime example) and also discusses
44:1934:108933:17:16:72:540:638:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:upwinding. Our numerical method adds to his in three dimensions. First and most obviously,
44:1935:109023:16:17:72:540:622:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we consider coupled HJB and KF equations rather than just the HJB equation in isolation:
44:1936:109111:17:17:72:540:605:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the system (7), (8) and (11) rather than just (7).  We show that there is a tight mapping
44:1937:109200:17:17:72:540:588:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:between the KF equation and the HJB equation and that this mapping can be conveniently
44:1938:109286:17:16:72:540:571:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:exploited in finite difference schemes (the adjoint/transpose property discussed in Section
44:1939:109377:16:17:72:540:555:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:3.4).  Candler, in contrast, does not discuss Kolmogorov Forward equations.  Second, even
44:1940:109466:17:17:72:540:538:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:when considered in isolation, our HJB equation differs from Candler’s and this is reflected
44:1941:109557:17:17:72:540:521:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in  the  solution  method.   In  particular,  we  show  how  to  handle  borrowing  constraints  by
44:1942:109656:17:16:72:540:504:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:mathematically casting them as state constraints (a step that requires using the notion of
44:1943:109746:16:17:72:540:488:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:viscosity solution) and designing an upwind method that respects these constraints.  Borrow-
44:1944:109838:17:17:72:540:471:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ing constraints are, of course, a ubiquitous feature of heterogeneous agent models.  Third,
44:1945:109929:17:17:72:540:454:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:we show that our solution method has well-developed theoretical underpinnings by making
44:1946:110016:17:20:72:438:437:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the connection to theBarles and Souganidis(1991) convergence theory.
44:1947:110084:20:16:89:539:417:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Besides  the  finite  difference  method,  there  are  many  alternative  methods  for  solving
44:1948:110179:16:17:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:partial differential  equations  in general  and  HJB  and  KF equations in particular.   Exam-
44:1949:110274:17:17:72:540:384:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ples include finite-element,  finite-volume,  semi-Lagrangean,  and Markov-chain approxima-
44:1950:110365:17:17:72:539:367:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tion methods as well as approximation via orthogonal (e.g.  Chebyshev) polynomials.49In
44:1951:110452:17:16:72:540:350:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:principle, these other methods can also be used to solve heterogeneous agent models of the
44:1952:110542:16:17:72:539:334:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:type discussed here; in particular by following the same Steps 1 to 3 laid out in Section3.2
44:1953:110634:17:20:72:434:317:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:but simply exchanging the solution method usedwithinSteps 1 and 2.
44:1954:110700:20:17:89:539:297:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:There  is  no  sense  in  which  the  finite  difference  method  laid  out  here  dominates  these
44:1955:110799:17:17:72:540:280:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:other methods.  In fact, some of these other methods may even be more accurate for coarse
44:1956:110888:17:16:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:discretizations of the value function and distribution.  We nevertheless prefer it over other
44:1957:110981:16:17:72:540:247:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:methods  for  two  reasons.   First,  the  finite  difference  method  is  transparent  and  easy  to
44:1958:111082:17:17:72:540:230:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:implement.  In case the algorithm spits out junk, it is usually easy to track down the prob-
44:1959:111174:17:17:72:539:213:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:lem.  Second, and as explained in Section3.4, the finite difference method delivers a useful
44:1960:111266:17:16:72:540:196:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:symmetry in the solution methods for the HJB and KF equations (that the matrix in the
44:1961:111351:16:17:72:540:180:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:latter  is  the  transpose  of  that  in  the  former).   Other  methods  typically  do  not  have  this
44:1962:111455:17:18:72:540:163:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:property.  Finally, because the finite difference method is fast, choosing fine grids is usually
44:1963:111551:18:16:81:539:145:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:49Some of these methods have also been used in economics.  For example,Golosov and Lucas(2007) and
44:1964:111649:16:11:72:539:129:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:Barczyk and Kredler(2014) use the Markov Chain approximation method ofKushner and Dupuis(2013).
44:1965:111744:11:12:71:539:118:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:Because some of our paper’s readers have wondered about this in the past, we should emphasize that a finite
44:1966:111851:12:12:71:539:106:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:difference method is different from the Kushner-Dupuis method:  their approach essentially transforms the
44:1967:111956:12:0:71:520:94:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:continuous-time problem into a discrete-time problem and then solves it using value function iteration.
45:1968:112061:0:16:72:540:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:not  costly  and  hence  it  is  not  a  concern  that  other  methods  may  be  more  accurate  with
45:1969:112162:16:47:72:183:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:coarse discretizations.
45:1970:112185:47:32:72:438:657:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:C:4    Generalizations and Other Applications
45:1971:112228:32:16:72:540:625:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Sections 4.1 and 4.2 discuss generalizations of the baseline Huggett model we have analyzed
45:1972:112319:16:17:72:539:609:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:thus  far.   Sections4.3to4.6present  a  number  of  other  theories,  mostly  to  showcase  the
45:1973:112415:17:41:72:294:592:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:portability of our computational algorithm.
45:1974:112458:41:27:72:336:551:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:4.1    More General Income Processes
45:1975:112494:27:17:72:539:524:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Our baseline model assumed that incomeyttakes one of two values, high and low.  We now
45:1976:112580:17:17:72:539:507:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:extend many of our results to an environment with a continuum of productivity types.50In
45:1977:112668:17:16:72:539:490:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:particular, the computational algorithm laid out in Section3 carries over without change.
45:1978:112757:16:17:72:540:474:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:This is true even though the system of equations describing an equilibrium will be a system
45:1979:112848:17:20:72:277:457:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of PDEs rather than a system of ODEs.
45:1980:112885:20:17:89:539:437:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:As  in  Section1.1,  there  is  a  continuum  of  individuals  that  are  heterogeneous  in  their
45:1981:112983:17:16:72:540:420:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wealthaand incomey.  The state of the economy is the joint distribution of income and
45:1982:113068:16:17:72:539:404:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wealthg(a,y,t).  The simplest way of introducing a continuum of income types is to work

45:1987:113446:17:17:71:539:292:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Wiener process or standard Brownian motion and the functionsμandσare called the drift
45:1988:113531:17:17:71:539:275:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and the diffusion of the process.  We normalize the process such that its stationary mean
45:1989:113620:17:16:71:539:258:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:equals one.  An individual’s problem is now to maximize (1) subject to (2),  (3) and (54),
45:1990:113710:16:20:71:391:242:12:g_d0_f6:8:g_d0_f7:B:taking as given the evolution of the interest ratertfort≥0.52

48:2104:119121:0:16:72:539:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Capital depreciates at rateδ.  There is a representative firm with a constant returns to scale
48:2105:119215:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:production functionY=F(K,L).  Since factor markets are competitive, the wage and the

48:2108:119356:31:19:72:526:625:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:where we use that the mean of the stationary distribution of productivitieszequals one.
48:2109:119443:19:17:89:539:606:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:Because the income fluctuation problem at the heart of the Aiyagari model is the same
48:2110:119528:17:17:72:539:589:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:as  that  in  the  Huggett  model  all  of  Propositions1  to3  apply  without  change.   So  does
48:2111:119626:17:17:72:539:572:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Proposition4.  Proposition5 applies by exploiting a homogeneity property noted byAuclert
48:2112:119714:17:16:72:539:555:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and Rognlie(2016), namely that individual policy functions and therefore aggregate saving
48:2113:119803:16:17:72:539:539:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:is  homothetic  in  the  wage  rate,S(r,w)  =wS(r,1)  for  allw >0.54The  computational
48:2114:119890:17:17:71:539:522:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:algorithm is again unchanged except that, in Step 3, it imposes (60) and (61) rather than
48:2115:119979:17:40:71:95:505:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(11).
48:2116:119984:40:28:71:558:465:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:4.3    Non-Convexities: Indivisible Housing, Mortgages, Poverty Traps
48:2117:120053:28:17:71:540:437:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:An important class of economic theories involves non-convexities as inSkiba(1978). Another
48:2118:120143:17:17:71:539:420:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:important  class  features  prices  in  financial  constraints  as  inKiyotaki  and  Moore(1997).
48:2119:120240:17:16:71:539:403:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:We  here  provide  a  parsimonious  example  of  a  theory  that  features  both  model  elements:
48:2120:120338:16:17:71:539:387:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:individuals can take out a mortgage to buy houses subject to a down-payment constraint
48:2121:120424:17:17:71:539:370:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:– hence the price in the constraint – and housing is indivisible – hence the non-convexity.
48:2122:120515:17:17:71:539:353:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The purpose of this subsection is not to propose a quantitatively realistic model of housing;
48:2123:120608:17:16:71:539:336:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:rather it is to showcase what kind of models can be solved with our computational algorithm.

52:2241:127066:17:17:72:540:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:transition dynamics.  Not surprisingly given the discussion thus far,  the economy features
52:2242:127157:17:17:72:540:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:history dependence in the sense that initial conditions determine where the economy ends
52:2243:127245:17:16:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:up in the long run.  As already noted, the point of this subsection is not to argue quantita-
52:2244:127338:16:17:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tively that the presence of indivisible housing and down-payment constraints creates history
52:2245:127430:17:41:72:513:603:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:dependence.  Rather it is to showcase the possibilities of our computational algorithm.
52:2246:127517:41:27:72:170:562:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:4.4    Fat Tails
52:2247:127533:27:17:72:540:535:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:As shown in Proposition3, the stationary wealth distribution in the Huggett economy with a
52:2248:127623:17:17:72:540:518:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:bounded income process is bounded above.  More generally, any income process with a thin-
52:2249:127712:17:16:72:540:501:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tailed  stationary  distribution  results  in  a  thin-tailed  wealth  distribution.55This  property
52:2250:127812:16:17:72:540:485:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:of the model is,  of course,  problematic vis-`a-vis wealth distributions observed in the data
52:2251:127906:17:17:72:539:468:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:which are typically heavily skewed and feature fat upper tails.  In Online AppendixE.2we
52:2252:127994:17:17:72:540:451:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:show how to extend the Huggett model of Section1 to feature a fat-tailed stationary wealth
52:2253:128084:17:17:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:distribution.  We do this by introducing a risky asset in addition to the riskless bond.  The
52:2254:128177:17:16:72:540:417:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:insight that the introduction of “investment risk” into a Bewley model generates a Pareto
52:2255:128266:16:17:72:540:401:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:tail for the wealth distribution is due toBenhabib, Bisin, and Zhu(2015) and our argument
52:2256:128355:17:41:72:499:384:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:mimics several of their steps.  Also seeJones(2015) andDe Nardi and Fella(2017).
52:2257:128435:41:27:72:389:343:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:4.5    Multiple Assets with Adjustment Costs
52:2258:128479:27:17:72:539:316:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:The  models  discussed  in  Sections4.3and4.4featured  two  assets:  bonds  and  housing  in
52:2259:128571:17:17:72:540:299:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the former;  bonds and a risky asset in the latter.  But in both cases portfolio adjustment
52:2260:128662:17:16:72:540:282:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:between  the  two  assets  was  costless  and  we  could  therefore  collapse  the  two  assets  into
52:2261:128763:16:17:72:540:266:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:one  state  variable,  net  worth.   Costless  portfolio  adjustment  may  be  a  bad  assumption
52:2262:128860:17:17:72:540:249:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:in  many  applications.   For  instance,  buying  a  house  may  entail  both  fixed  and  variable
52:2263:128959:17:17:72:540:232:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:transaction  costs.   The  same  is  true  for  illiquid  assets  more  generally.   For  example,  an
52:2264:129061:17:16:72:539:215:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:individual withdrawing funds from her retirement account typically incurs a penalty.Kaplan,
52:2265:129152:16:17:72:539:199:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Moll, and Violante(2016) show how to extend the computational algorithm developed here
52:2266:129238:17:17:72:540:182:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to  handle  multiple  assets  with  kinked  (but  convex)  adjustment  costs  and  argue  that  the
52:2267:129337:17:18:72:535:165:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:two-asset structure is important for understanding the monetary transmission mechanism.
52:2268:129424:18:15:81:539:147:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:55As discussed byBenhabib and Bisin(2016) the only exception to this is if income itself has a stationary
52:2269:129529:15:12:72:540:132:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:distribution with a fat (Pareto) tail.  However, this assumption would generate the counterfactual prediction
52:2270:129638:12:12:72:540:120:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:that the tail of the wealth distribution is equally fat as that of the income distribution.  In the data, instead,
52:2271:129752:12:0:72:432:108:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:the wealth distribution has a considerably fatter tail than the income distribution.
53:2272:129838:0:27:72:285:720:14:g_d0_f5:14:g_d0_f5:C:4.6    Stopping Time Problems
53:2273:129867:27:17:72:540:693:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:In models with multiple assets like the one discussed above, one may want to allow for non-
53:2274:129958:17:17:72:540:676:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:convex adjustment costs, e.g. fixed costs. In work in progress we show how our algorithm also
53:2275:130051:17:17:72:540:659:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:generalizes to solve such problems.  Non-convex adjustment costs result in the individual’s
53:2276:130142:17:16:72:540:642:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:problem becoming a stopping time problem rather than a standard dynamic optimization
53:2277:130226:16:17:72:540:626:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:problem (Stokey, 2009).  The value function then no longer solves an HJB equation; instead
53:2278:130316:17:17:72:539:609:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:it solves a so-called “HJB Variational Inequality” (Øksendal,1995;Tourin,2013).56Nev-
53:2279:130401:17:17:71:539:592:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:ertheless,  the  algorithm  developed  here  can  be  generalized  in  a  relatively  straightforward
53:2280:130502:17:16:71:540:575:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:manner  to  solve  these  type  of  stopping  time  problems.57The  computational  method  for
53:2281:130596:16:17:71:539:559:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:solving stopping time problems also promises to be useful in other applications, e.g.  prob-
53:2282:130688:17:17:71:540:542:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:lems  involving  default  by  individuals  (see  e.g.Livshits,  MacGee,  and  Tertilt,2007)  or  by
53:2283:130787:17:17:71:539:525:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:sovereign states (see e.g.Aguiar, Amador, Farhi, and Gopinath,2013, for a continuous-time
53:2284:130876:17:46:72:139:508:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:formulation).
53:2285:130889:46:32:72:194:462:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:C:5    Conclusion
53:2286:130904:32:17:72:540:430:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:This  paper  makes  two  types  of  contributions.   First,  we  prove  a  number  of  new  theoreti-
53:2287:131005:17:16:72:540:413:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:cal results about the Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model, the workhorse theory of income and
53:2288:131092:16:17:72:540:397:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:wealth distribution in macroeconomics:  (i) an analytic characterization of the consumption
53:2289:131183:17:17:72:540:380:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:and saving behavior of the poor, particularly their marginal propensities to consume; (ii) a
53:2290:131275:17:17:72:540:363:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:closed-form solution for the wealth distribution in a special case with two income types; (iii)
53:2291:131370:17:16:72:540:346:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:a proof that there is a unique stationary equilibrium if the intertemporal elasticity of sub-
53:2292:131463:16:17:72:540:330:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stitution is weakly greater than one; (iv) a characterization of “soft” borrowing constraints.
53:2293:131557:17:17:72:540:313:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:Second, we develop a simple, efficient and portable algorithm for numerically solving both
53:2294:131647:17:17:72:540:296:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:stationary equilibria and transition dynamics of a wide class of heterogeneous agent models,
53:2295:131739:17:16:72:540:279:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:including – but not limited to – this model.  Both types of contributions were made possible
53:2296:131831:16:17:72:540:263:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:by recasting the Aiyagari-Bewley-Huggett model in continuous time, thereby transforming
53:2297:131918:17:18:72:361:246:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:the model into a system of partial differential equations.
53:2298:131976:18:16:81:539:228:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:56Economists typically tackle these types of problems by splitting the state space into an inaction region
53:2299:132082:16:12:72:540:212:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:and an adjustment region,  postulate that the value function satisfies an HJB equation in the adjustment
53:2300:132186:12:12:72:540:200:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:region and a “smooth pasting” condition on the boundary.  This approach is restrictive:  in one dimension,
53:2301:132292:12:11:72:540:188:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:the boundary of the inaction region is just a threshold; but as soon as there is more than one state variable, it
53:2302:132405:11:12:72:540:177:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:becomes exceedingly hard to find the boundaries of the adjustment region.  The approach is even somewhat
53:2303:132509:12:12:72:540:165:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:misleading:   using  the  HJB  Variational  Inequality  formulation  the  smooth  pasting  condition  is  a  result
53:2304:132624:12:12:72:539:153:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:rather than an exogenously imposed axiom (Øksendal,1995).  Solving stopping time problems in multiple
53:2305:132725:12:9:71:423:141:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:dimensions then poses no conceptual problem over solving one-dimensional ones.
53:2306:132803:9:15:81:539:132:7:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f9:F:57See   the   applications   labelled   “Stopping   Time   Problem”   herehttp://www.princeton.edu/∼moll/
53:2307:132908:15:12:72:540:117:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:HACTproject.htm,  particularly  a  simple  problem  of  exercising  an  option  borrowed  fromStokey(2009)
53:2308:133014:12:12:72:539:105:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:http://www.princeton.edu/∼moll/HACTproject/optionsimple.pdf  and  a  model  with  two  assets  and  non-
53:2309:133118:12:0:72:520:93:10:g_d0_f9:10:g_d0_f9:F:convex adjustment costs http://www.princeton.edu/∼moll/HACTproject/liquidilliquidnumerical.pdf.
54:2310:133215:0:16:89:539:720:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:A:It  is  our  hope  that  the  methods  developed  in  this  paper,  particularly  the  numerical
54:2311:133311:16:17:72:540:704:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:algorithm, will also prove useful in other applications.  One potential application is to spatial
54:2312:133408:17:17:72:540:687:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:theories  of  trade  and  development  as  in  Rossi-Hansberg  (2005)  andAllen  and  Arkolakis
54:2313:133503:17:17:72:540:670:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:(2014). These theories typically feature a continuum of producers and households distributed
54:2314:133595:17:17:72:540:653:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:over a continuum of locations.  In dynamic versions,  space would simply be an additional
54:2315:133684:17:16:72:540:636:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:variable in the HJB and KF equations.  A challenge would be how to solve for equilibrium
54:2316:133772:16:17:72:540:620:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:prices which are typically functions of space rather than a small number of (potentially time-
54:2317:133866:17:17:72:540:603:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:varying) scalars.  Related, a second avenue for future research is to explore richer interactions
54:2318:133963:17:17:72:540:586:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:between individuals.  In the class of theories we have considered here, individuals interact
54:2319:134055:17:16:72:540:569:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:only through prices. But for many questions of interest, richer interactions may be important:
54:2320:134149:16:17:72:540:553:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:for instance there may be more “local” interactions in the form of knowledge spillovers or
54:2321:134239:17:17:72:539:536:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:diffusion (see e.g.Perla and Tonetti,2014;Lucas and Moll,2014;Burger, Lorz, and Wolfram,
54:2322:134327:17:17:72:539:519:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:2016;Benhabib, Perla, and Tonetti,2017).  In principle, the apparatus put forward in this
54:2323:134416:17:17:72:540:502:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:paper – the Mean Field Game system of coupled HJB and KF equations – is general enough
54:2324:134502:17:42:72:287:485:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:B:to encompass richer models such as these.
54:2325:134543:42:24:72:163:443:17:g_d0_f5:17:g_d0_f5:C:References
